                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Banana cultivars mainly involve <i>M. acuminata</i> (A genome) and <i>Musa balbisiana</i> (B genome) and are sometimes diploid but generally triploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Simmonds, N. W. The Evolution of the Bananas 101–131 (Longman, 1962)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1347">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="D’Hont, A., Paget-Goy, A., Escoute, J. &amp; Carreel, F. The interspecific genome structure of cultivated banana, Musa spp. revealed by genomic DNA in situ hybridization. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 177–183 (2000)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1350">6</a></sup>. We sequenced the genome of DH-Pahang, a doubled-haploid <i>M. acuminata</i> genotype (2<i>n</i> = 22), of the subspecies <i>malaccensis</i> that contributed one of the three <i>acuminata</i> genomes of Cavendish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Raboin, L. M. et al. Diploid ancestors of triploid export banana cultivars: molecular identification of 2n restitution gamete donors and n gamete donors. Mol. Breed. 16, 333–341 (2005)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1367">7</a></sup>. A total of 27.5 million Roche/454 single reads and 2.1 million Sanger reads were produced, representing 20.5× coverage of the 523-megabase (Mb) DH-Pahang genome size, as estimated by flow cytometry. In addition, 50× of Illumina data were used to correct sequence errors. The assembly consisted of 24,425 contigs and 7,513 scaffolds with a total length of 472.2 Mb, which represented 90% of the estimated DH-Pahang genome size. Ninety per cent of the assembly was in 647 scaffolds, and the N50 (the scaffold size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) was 1.3 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 1–3</a>). We anchored 70% of the assembly (332 Mb) along the 11 <i>Musa</i> linkage groups of the Pahang genetic map. This corresponded to 258 scaffolds and included 98.0% of the scaffolds larger than 1 Mb and 92% of the annotated genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>We identified 36,542 protein-coding gene models in the <i>Musa</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 1 and 5</a>). A total of 235 microRNAs from 37 families were identified, including only one of the eight microRNA gene (<i>MIR</i>) families found so far solely in Poaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Cuperus, J. T., Fahlgren, N. &amp; Carrington, J. C. Evolution and functional diversification of MIRNA genes. Plant Cell 23, 431–442 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1402">8</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 6 and 7</a>).</p><p>Viral sequences related to the banana streak virus (BSV) dsDNA plant pararetrovirus were found to be integrated in the Pahang genome, with 24 loci spanning 10 chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). They belonged to a badnavirus phylogenetic group that differed from the endogenous BSV species (eBSV) found in <i>M. balbisiana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gayral, P. &amp; Iskra-Caruana, M. L. Phylogeny of banana streak virus reveals recent and repetitive endogenization in the genome of its banana host (Musa spp.). J. Mol. Evol. 69, 65–80 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1420">9</a></sup> and most of them formed a new subgroup (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Importantly, all of the integrations were highly reorganized and fragmented and thus did not seem to be capable of forming free infectious viral particles, contrary to the eBSV described in <i>M. balbisiana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Iskra-Caruana, M. L., Baurens, F. C., Gayral, P. &amp; Chabannes, M. A four-partner plant–virus interaction: enemies can also come from within. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 23, 1394–1402 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1430">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Transposable elements account for almost half of the <i>Musa</i> sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 1 and 8–10</a>). Long terminal repeat retrotransposons represent the largest part, with <i>Copia</i> elements being much more abundant than <i>Gypsy</i> elements (25.7–11.6%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). No major recent wave of long terminal repeat retrotransposon insertions appears to have occurred in the <i>Musa</i> lineage. Fewer than 1% of the long terminal repeat retrotransposons are complete and their median date of insertion is around 4 Myr ago, corresponding to the half-life of this type of transposable element<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860–869 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1459">11</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Long interspersed elements (LINEs) represent 5.5% of the genome. The banana genome is exceptional in the composition of its class 2 element population, which represents only about 1.3% of the genome. The only superfamilies identified were <i>hAT</i>, followed by <i>Harbinger</i> and <i>Mutator</i>. Only the first family was significantly represented and had non-autonomous deletion derivatives. The superfamilies CACTA and <i>Mariner</i>, which have been found in high copy numbers in all angiosperm genomes studied so far, are absent from the banana genome. Gene-rich regions are mostly located on distal parts of chromosomes, as observed in other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). There is, however, a particularly sharp transition between gene-rich and transposable-element-rich regions. This observation is confirmed by the pattern observed after genomic <i>in situ</i> hybridization, which shows that transposable elements are typically concentrated around centromeres in <i>Musa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Jeridi, M. et al. Homoeologous chromosome pairing between the A and B genomes of Musa spp. revealed by genomic in situ hybridization. Ann. Bot. (Lond.) 108, 975–981 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1491">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). The asymmetric transposable element distributions along the chromosomes indicated that chromosomes 1 and 2 are acrocentric in DH-Pahang (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Long terminal repeat retrotransposons are particularly abundant in centromeric and pericentromeric chromosome regions. Their accumulation in these regions, particularly for the oldest ones, suggests that they are preferentially eliminated from gene-rich regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1501">13</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Remarkably, typical short tandem centromeric repeats were not found in <i>Musa</i>. However, one long interspersed element (named <i>Nanica</i>) identified in the unassembled reads was localized by fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization in the centromeric region of all <i>Musa</i> chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="&#xA;                        Chromosomal distribution of the main &#xA;                        M. acuminata&#xA;                         genome features.&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: 
                        <b>Chromosomal distribution of the main </b>
                        <b><i>M. acuminata</i></b>
                        <b> genome features.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="228" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Distribution of genes and transposable elements (left) and paralogous relationships between the 11 chromosomes indicated with 12 distinct colours corresponding to the 12 <i>Musa</i> α/β ancestral blocks (right). LINEs, long interspersed elements.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM250">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Whole-genome duplications (WGDs) have played a major role in angiosperm genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J. A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1570">14</a></sup>; the first evidence of a WGD event in the <i>Musa</i> lineage was reported by Lescot <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lescot, M. et al. Insights into the Musa genome: syntenic relationships to rice and between Musa species. BMC Genomics 9, 58 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1579">15</a></sup>. We uncovered a complex pattern of paralogous relationships between the 11 <i>Musa</i> chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Most paralogous gene clusters shared relationships with three other clusters, suggesting that two WGDs (denoted as α and β) occurred (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). Based on <i>Ks</i> and synteny relationships, duplicated gene clusters were tentatively assembled into 12 <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks representing the ancestral genome before the α/β duplications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig1">Figs 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Figs 10–12</a>). The duplicated segments included in the <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks cover 222 Mb (67% of the anchored assembly) and contain 26,829 genes (80% of the anchored genes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 11</a>). The <i>Ks</i> distribution among pairs of paralogous gene clusters dated the two WGDs at a similar period around 65 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>), consistent with the WGDs that occurred in many different plant lineages near the Cretaceous–Tertiary boundary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J. A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680–688 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1624">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). Additional paralogous relationships between the 12 <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks displaying higher <i>Ks</i> values suggested that an additional, more ancient duplication event (denoted as γ) occurred around 100 Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Figs 10, 11, 13 and 14</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="&#xA;                        Whole-genome duplication events.&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b>Whole-genome duplication events.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="610" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Paralogous relationships between chromosome segments from <i>Musa</i> α/β ancestral blocks 2 (red) and 8 (green). The 12 <i>Musa</i> α/β ancestral blocks are shown in different colours on the circle. <b>b</b>, Orthologous relationships of <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks 2 and 8 with rice ancestral blocks ρ2, ρ5 and σ6. We did not observe a one-to-one relationship between, for instance, <i>Musa</i> α/β ancestral block 2 and one ρ ancestral block, which suggests that the γ and σ duplications are two separate events. <b>c</b>, Representation of the deduced WGD event.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM251">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="&#xA;                        Timing of whole-genome duplications relative to speciation events within representative monocotyledons and eudicotyledons.&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b>Timing of whole-genome duplications relative to speciation events within representative monocotyledons and eudicotyledons.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="579" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Boxes indicate WGD events. Green boxes indicate WGD events analysed in this paper. All nodes have 100% bootstrap support in a maximum likelihood analysis. Branch lengths (synonymous substitution rate) are indicated. The timing of the β WGD event relative to the Musaceae/Zingiberaceae split remains to be clarified.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM252">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the grass lineage, it is well established that one WGD (denoted as ρ) occurred around 50–70 Myr ago, after Poales separated from other monocotyledon orders<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Comparative genome analysis of monocots and dicots, toward characterization of angiosperm diversity. Curr. Opin. Biotechnol. 15, 120–125 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1728">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11–24 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1731">17</a></sup>. Evidence was reported on an additional WGD (denoted as σ) earlier in the monocotyledon lineage, but after its divergence from the eudicotyledons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1735">18</a></sup>. Our comparison of the <i>Musa</i> ancestral blocks with the Poaceae ρ and σ ancestral blocks as defined by Tang <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1744">18</a></sup> revealed that genes from segments of different ρ blocks (corresponding to one σ block) have orthologous relationships with the same <i>Musa</i> regions, showing that the σ Poaceae event is not shared with <i>Musa</i>. Reciprocally, genes from <i>Musa</i> α/β paralogous segments have orthologous relationships with the same ρ and σ regions, showing that the earliest duplication (γ) we identified in the <i>Musa</i> lineage is not shared with Poaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig2">Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>).</p><p>Independent phylogenomic analyses performed on 3,553 gene families, including genes mapped to syntenic ancestral blocks, generated further evidence (98.7–77.6% of the gene trees, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>) that the three rounds of palaeopolyploidization identified in the <i>Musa</i> genome and the two previously reported in the Poaceae lineage occurred independently after the Poales and Zingiberales divergence estimated at 109–123 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Magallon, S. &amp; Castillo, A. Angiosperm diversification through time. Am. J. Bot. 96, 349–365 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1776">19</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><p>Resolution of the Zingiberales relationship relative to Poales and Arecales (palms) has been problematic (see, for example, Givnish <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Givnish, T. J. et al. Assembling the tree of the monocotyledons: plastome sequence phylogeny and evolution of Poales. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 97, 584–616 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1791">20</a></sup>), but our analysis of 93 single-copy nuclear genes suggested that the palms are more closely related to Zingiberales (including <i>Musa</i>) than to Poales (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). Phylogenomic and synteny analyses indicated that the palms do not, however, share any of the Poales or Zingiberales WGDs discussed here (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Figs 17 and 18</a>). Moreover, our <i>Ks</i> analyses of date-palm gene models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nature Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1814">21</a></sup> indicated that the palm genome had its own WGD event (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>).</p><p>Most (65.4%) of the genes included in the <i>Musa</i> α/β ancestral blocks are singletons and only 10% are retained in four copies, in agreement with the loss of most gene-duplicated copies after WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schnable, J. C., Springer, N. M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069–4074 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1827">22</a></sup>. The most retained gene ontology categories corresponded to genes involved in transcription regulation (transcription factor activity), signal transduction including small GTPase-mediated signal transduction and protein kinases, and translational elongation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 12–14</a>). This might be explained by the gene balance hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Birchler, J. A., Riddle, N. C., Auger, D. L. &amp; Veitia, R. A. Dosage balance in gene regulation: biological implications. Trends Genet. 21, 219–226 (2005)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1837">23</a></sup>, which suggests that genes involved in multiproteic complexes or regulatory genes are dosage sensitive and thus are more prone to be co-retained or co-lost after WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Veitia, R. A., Bottani, S. &amp; Birchler, J. A. Cellular reactions to gene dosage imbalance: genomic, transcriptomic and proteomic effects. Trends Genet. 24, 390–397 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1842">24</a></sup>. With 3,155 genes, the number of <i>Musa</i> transcription factors identified is among the highest of all sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 15 and 16</a>).</p><p>Comparison of <i>Musa</i>, rice, sorghum, <i>Brachypodium</i>, date palm (<i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>) and <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteomes revealed 7,674 gene clusters in common to all six species, thus representing ancestral gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Interestingly, many specific clusters (2,809 in our setting) proved specific to Poaceae, suggesting a high level of gene divergence and diversification within the grass lineage. Specific <i>Musa</i> clusters (759) were enriched in genes encoding transcription factors (for example, <i>Myb</i> and <i>AP2</i>/<i>ERF</i> families), defence-related proteins, enzymes of cell-wall biosynthesis and enzymes of secondary metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 17</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Six-way Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families (sequence clusters) among  M. acuminata, P. dactylifera, Arabidopsis thaliana, Oryza sativa, Sorghum bicolor  and  Brachypodium distachyon  genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: <b>Six-way Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families (sequence clusters) among </b> <b><i>M. acuminata</i></b>, <b><i>P. dactylifera</i></b>, <b><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i></b>, <b><i>Oryza sativa</i></b>, <b><i>Sorghum bicolor</i></b> <b> and </b> <b><i>Brachypodium distachyon</i></b> <b> genomes.</b></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature11241/MediaObjects/41586_2012_Article_BFnature11241_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="513" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Numbers of clusters are provided in the intersections. The total number of sequences for each species is provided under the species name (total number of sequences/total number of clustered sequences).</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM253">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature11241/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We compared the distribution of GC3 content (G or C in the third codon position) in <i>Musa</i> coding sequences with those of rice, ginger (<i>Zingiber officinale</i>) and date palm because this distribution was shown to be bimodal in Poaceae and unimodal in all analysed eudicotyledons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Carels, N. &amp; Bernardi, G. Two classes of genes in plants. Genetics 154, 1819–1825 (2000)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1948">25</a></sup>. In <i>Musa</i>, a GC-rich peak was apparent but less distinct from the GC-poor one (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Figs 20–23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 18</a>), which confirms preliminary evidence that placed <i>Musa</i> in an intermediate position<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lescot, M. et al. Insights into the Musa genome: syntenic relationships to rice and between Musa species. BMC Genomics 9, 58 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1968">15</a></sup>. This feature was shared with ginger (Zingiberales) and contrasts with the unimodal GC distribution of date-palm coding sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>).</p><p>Plant conserved non-coding sequences (CNSs)—a type of phylogenetic footprint—are enriched in known transcription factors or other <i>cis</i>-acting binding sites, and are usually clustered around regulatory genes, supporting their functionality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Subramaniam, S. Conserved noncoding sequences (CNSs) in higher plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 12, 126–132 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d29429e1981">26</a></sup>. Starting with a collection of 16,978 CNSs conserved in Poaceae, we used the <i>Musa</i> genome to identify the 116 most deeply conserved regulatory binding sequences in the commelinid monocotyledon lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 19 and 20</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>). Deeply conserved CNSs in commelinids were frequently found located 5′ to genes encoding transcription factors, and were significantly enriched in WRKY motifs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 21</a>). After WGD, genes associated with deeply conserved CNSs were found to be retained as duplicates more often than genes with less deeply conserved CNSs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 22</a>). The banana genome also served as a stepping-stone to finding CNSs conserved beyond monocotyledons, including 18 CNSs that were found in this study to be conserved in the expected syntenic position in eudicotyledons as well (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 23</a>). This evolutionary distance is not unusual for vertebrate CNSs (detectable after more than 400 million years of divergence)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Woolfe, A. et al. Highly conserved non-coding sequences are associated with vertebrate development. PLoS Biol. 3, e7 (2005)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2007">27</a></sup> but it surpasses the findings of previous plant whole-genome surveys<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Freeling, M. &amp; Subramaniam, S. Conserved noncoding sequences (CNSs) in higher plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 12, 126–132 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2011">26</a></sup>. Plant deeply conserved CNSs are therefore rare but do exist, and are short compared with those of animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Woolfe, A. et al. Highly conserved non-coding sequences are associated with vertebrate development. PLoS Biol. 3, e7 (2005)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2016">27</a></sup>, and must be at least as old as monocotyledon–eudicotyledon divergence (more than 130 million years of divergence).</p><p>The reference <i>Musa</i> genome sequence represents a major advance in the quest to unravel the complex genetics of this vital crop, whose breeding is particularly challenging. Having access to the entire <i>Musa</i> gene repertoire is a key to identifying genes responsible for important agronomic characters, such as fruit quality and pest resistance. Bananas are exported green and then ripened by application of ethylene. RNA-Seq analysis indicated strong transcriptional reprogramming in mature green banana fruits after ethylenic treatment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Tables 24–26</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>). Transcription factors were particularly involved with 597 differentially regulated genes. Various modifications confirmed the biochemistry of the banana ripening process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Fils-Lycaon, B. et al. Acid invertase as a serious candidate to control the balance sucrose versus (glucose plus fructose) of banana fruit during ripening. Sci. Hortic. (Amsterdam) 129, 197–206 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2039">28</a></sup>, such as highly upregulated genes encoding cell-wall modifying enzymes, three downregulated starch synthase genes and one upregulated β-amylase gene. Two WGD-derived paralogous vacuolar invertase genes involved in sucrose conversion displayed opposite expression profiles, suggesting subfunctionalization and possible contribution to the soluble sugar balance in ripening bananas (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 26</a>). The race against pathogen evolution is particularly critical in clonally propagated crops such as banana. Up to 50 pesticide treatments a year are required in large plantations against black leaf streak disease, a recent pandemy caused by <i>Mycosphaerella fijiensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De Lapeyre de Bellaire, L., Fouré, E., Abadie, C. &amp; Carlier, J. Black leaf streak disease is challenging the banana industry. Fruits 65, 327–342 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2048">3</a></sup>. Moreover, outbreaks of a new race of the devastating Panama disease fungus (<i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> f. sp. <i>cubense</i>) are spreading in Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Dita, M. A., Waalwijk, C., Buddenhagen, I. W., Souza, M. T. &amp; Kema, G. H. J. A molecular diagnostic for tropical race 4 of the banana fusarium wilt pathogen. Plant Pathol. 59, 348–357 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2059">4</a></sup>. Among defence-related genes, those encoding nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat proteins were found to be little represented in the <i>Musa</i> sequence (89 genes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 27</a>). RNA-Seq analysis showed that receptor-like kinase genes were upregulated in a partially resistant interaction with <i>M. fijiensis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 28</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>). Interestingly, direct links between basal plant immunity triggered by receptor-like kinase proteins and quantitative trait loci for partial resistance have been recently established in several plant species (see, for example, Poland <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Poland, J. A., Bradbury, P. J., Buckler, E. S. &amp; Nelson, R. J. Genome-wide nested association mapping of quantitative resistance to northern leaf blight in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 6893–6898 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2084">29</a></sup>). In addition, we showed that DH-Pahang is highly resistant to the new broad-range <i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> race 4 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 28</a>), thus conferring additional specific value to the DH-Pahang sequence.</p><p>The <i>Musa</i> genome sequence reported here bridges a large gap in genome evolution studies. As such, it sheds new light on the monocotyledon lineage. Several Poaceae-specific characteristics could be highlighted, boosting prospects for analysing the emergence of this very successful family. The <i>Musa</i> genome also enabled identification of deeply conserved CNS within commelinid monocotyledons and between monocotyledons and eudicotyledons, representing an invaluable resource for detecting novel motifs with a gene regulation function. We detected three rounds of polyploidization in the <i>Musa</i> lineage, which were followed by gene loss and chromosome rearrangements, resulting in little synteny conservation between lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Figs 29 and 30</a>) and over-retention of some gene classes, thus providing ample opportunities for independent diversification. In particular, transcription factor families are strikingly expanded in <i>Musa</i> compared with other plant genomes and probably contribute to specific aspects of banana development.</p><p>The <i>Musa</i> genome sequence is therefore an important advance towards securing food supplies from new generations of <i>Musa</i> crops, and provides an invaluable stepping-stone for plant gene and genome evolution studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>Sanger (ABI 3730xl sequencers) and Roche/454 (GSFLX pyrosequencing platform) reads were assembled with Newbler. Scaffolds were anchored to Pahang linkage groups using 652 markers (SSR and DArT). Protein-coding gene model prediction on the repeat-masked sequence was done with the GAZE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Howe, K. L., Chothia, T. &amp; Durbin, R. GAZE: a generic framework for the integration of gene-prediction data by dynamic programming. Genome Res. 12, 1418–1427 (2002)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2132">30</a></sup> computational framework by combining <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, protein similarity, existing banana and monocotyledon transcript information and banana RNA-Seq data. A reference library of <i>Musa</i> transposable elements was built based on sequence similarity at the protein and nucleic acid levels and on searches for transposable-element structural signatures. The library was used with the REPET package (<a href="http://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Tools/REPET">http://urgi.versailles.inra.fr/Tools/REPET</a>) to screen the <i>Musa</i> assembly and quantify repeats.</p><p>RNA-Seq differential gene expression analysis was performed using Illumina GAIIx 76 bases reads that were mapped to the DH-Pahang sequence using SOAP2 (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Online Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Online Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Plant material and DNA preparation</h3><p>Doubled-haploid Pahang (DH-Pahang, ITC1511) was obtained from wild <i>M. acuminata</i> subspecies <i>malaccensis</i> accession ‘Pahang’ through anther culture and spontaneous chromosome doubling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bakry, F., Assani, A. &amp; Kerbellec, F. Haploid induction: androgenesis in Musa balbisiana . Fruits 63, 45–49 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2180">31</a></sup>. Genome sizes were estimated by flow cytometry according to Marie and Brown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Marie, D. &amp; Brown, S. C. A cytometric exercise in plant DNA histograms, with 2C values for 70 species. Biol. Cell 78, 41–51 (1993)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2184">32</a></sup>. High molecular weight DNA was prepared from the youngest fully expanded leaf of DH-Pahang as described in Piffanelli <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Piffanelli, P., Vilarinhos, A., Safar, J., Sabau, X. &amp; Dolezel, J. Construction of bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) libaries of banana (Musa acuminata and Musa balbisiana). Fruits 63, 375–379 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2190">33</a></sup> with minor modifications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome sequencing</h3><p>The genome was sequenced using a Whole Genome Shotgun strategy combining Sanger, Roche/454 GSFLX and Illumina GAIIx technologies. Sanger sequencing was performed with the ABI 3730xl on 10-kilobase (kb) inserts and on two BAC libraries generated with the HindIII and BamHI restriction enzymes resulting in 2.0 million 10-kb fragment-ends and about 90,500 BAC-ends. A total of 27.5 million reads were obtained using Roche/454 GSFLX.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome assembly and automatic error corrections with Solexa/Illumina reads</h3><p>All reads were assembled with Newbler version MapAsmResearch-03/15/2010. From the initial 29,620,875 reads, 87.8% were assembled. We obtained 24,425 contigs that were linked into 7,513 scaffolds. The contig N50 (the contig size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly is included) was 43.1 kb, and the scaffold N50 was 1.3 Mb. The cumulative scaffold size was 472.2 Mb, about 10% smaller than the estimated genome size of 523 Mb. Sequence quality of scaffolds from the Newbler assembly was improved as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Aury, J. M. et al. High quality draft sequences for prokaryotic genomes using a mix of new sequencing technologies. BMC Genomics 9, 603 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2214">34</a></sup>, by automatic error corrections with Solexa/Illumina reads (50-fold genome coverage), which have a different bias in error type compared with 454 reads. To validate the assembly, we built a unigene set corresponding to 15,017 isotigs that were obtained from the assembly with Newbler (version MapAsmResearch-03/15/2010) of Roche/454 GSFLX reads from six different complementary DNA (cDNA) libraries (829,587 reads, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>). The unigenes were aligned with the assembly using the BLAT algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2221">35</a></sup> with default parameters, and the best match was kept for each unigene. The assembly covers a very large proportion of the euchromatin of the <i>M. acuminata</i> genome, as 99% of the set of 15,017 unigenes was recovered in the DH-Pahang genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Construction of the Pahang genetic map and sequence anchoring</h3><p>A genetic map was specifically developed for scaffold anchoring and orientation. A total of 2,454 single sequence repeats (SSR) markers and 1,008 polymorphic diversity array technology (DArT) markers were analysed including 1,411 new SSRs defined on sequence contigs and scaffolds. The map used for anchoring was built with 589 SSR and 63 DArT markers that were genotyped on 180 individuals of the Pahang self progeny. Data were analysed using JoinMap 4 (Plant Research International). The 652 markers anchored 258 scaffolds along the 11 linkage groups of the genetic map. Orientation of scaffolds was possible when two or more separated genetic markers were present on the same scaffold. All these data were used to generate 11 banana pseudo-chromosomes with 100Ns inserted between neighbouring scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene prediction</h3><p>The following resources were integrated to automatically build <i>Musa acuminata</i> gene models using GAZE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Howe, K. L., Chothia, T. &amp; Durbin, R. GAZE: a generic framework for the integration of gene-prediction data by dynamic programming. Genome Res. 12, 1418–1427 (2002)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2254">30</a></sup>: <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions from Geneid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Parra, G., Blanco, E. &amp; Guigo, R. GeneID in Drosophila . Genome Res. 10, 511–515 (2000)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2261">36</a></sup>, SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2265">37</a></sup> and FGENESH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2270">38</a></sup>; Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2274">39</a></sup> alignments of the UniProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bairoch, A. et al. The Universal Protein Resource (UniProt). Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D154–D159 (2005)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2278">40</a></sup> database; Est2genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Mott, R. EST_GENOME: a program to align spliced DNA sequences to unspliced genomic DNA. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 477–478 (1997)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2282">41</a></sup> alignments of full-length cDNAs from six tissue samples of DH-Pahang and a collection of 6,888,879 monocotyledon messenger RNAs from the EMBL database and finally Gmorse models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Denoeud, F. et al. Annotating genomes with massive-scale RNA sequencing. Genome Biol. 9, R175 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2286">42</a></sup> derived from RNA-Seq reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>). MicroRNAs were predicted based on comparison using the Plant MicroRNA Database (<a href="http://bioinformatics.cau.edu.cn/PMRD/">http://bioinformatics.cau.edu.cn/PMRD/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Identification of integrated pararetrovirus sequences</h3><p>Viral integrants in the DH-Pahang genome were detected with a BLASTN analysis using either full-length BSV sequences or a 540-base-pair fragment of the RT/RNase H region of the badnaviruses genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Identification, classification and distribution of <i>Musa</i> transposable elements</h3><p><i>Musa</i> transposable elements were identified based on sequence similarity at the protein and nucleic-acid levels using BLASTP and TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2326">43</a></sup> and by <i>de novo</i> identification based on transposable-element structural signatures. Repeats from 1,832,094 remaining unassembled reads were characterized with a BLASTN ‘walking’ approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao . Nature Genet. 43, 101–108 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2333">44</a></sup>. The obtained reference <i>Musa</i> transposable-element library was used with REPET<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Flutre, T., Duprat, E., Feuillet, C. &amp; Quesneville, H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS ONE 6, e16526 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2340">45</a></sup> to screen the assembly and quantify repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>). Insertion dates of full-length long terminal repeat retrotransposons were determined as described in Ma <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860–869 (2004)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2350">46</a></sup> with a substitution rate of 9 × 10<sup>−9</sup> per site per year, which is twofold higher than that determined for <i>Musa</i> genes by Lescot <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Lescot, M. et al. Insights into the Musa genome: syntenic relationships to rice and between Musa species. BMC Genomics 9, 58 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2361">15</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Identification of <i>Musa</i> WGDs and comparative genome analyses</h3><p>For the identification of <i>Musa</i> WGD, an all-against-all comparison of <i>Musa</i> proteins was done using the GenomeQuest BLAST package (LASSAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Glemet, E. &amp; Codani, J. J. LASSAP, a LArge Scale Sequence compArison Package. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 137–143 (1997)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2383">47</a></sup>) and retaining ten best hits for each gene. Clusters of paralogues composed of at least 20 genes with a maximal distance of 40 genes between syntenic genes were built with an in-house perl script, using a single linkage clustering with a Euclidian distance based on the gene index order in each chromosome. These clusters were refined using Synmap (<a href="http://synteny.cnr.berkeley.edu/CoGe/SynMap.pl">http://synteny.cnr.berkeley.edu/CoGe/SynMap.pl</a>) with the BLASTZ algorithm, an average distance expected between syntenic genes of 10, a maximum distance between two matches of 30, a minimum number of aligned gene pairs of 10 and a quota-align ratio of 3 to 3 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>).</p><p>For comparative genome analyses, orthologous gene-pairs were identified using predicted proteomes of <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> (IRGPS/RAP, build 4), <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (<a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/Download/Projets/Projet_ML/data/12X/annotation/">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/Download/Projets/Projet_ML/data/12X/annotation/</a>) and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> (draft sequence version 3, <a href="http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome/download.html">http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome/download.html</a>). Alignments were performed using BLASTP (e value 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) and retaining best hits. Syntenic clusters of genes were built using a single linkage clustering with a Euclidian distance. Dot-plots were performed using an in-house perl program allowing the painting of paralogous and orthologous gene clusters. Circle diagrams were made with Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2429">48</a></sup>.</p><p>To calculate the number of synonymous substitutions per site (<i>Ks</i>), ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Larkin, M. A. et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947–2948 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2439">49</a></sup> alignments of paralogous and orthologous protein sequences were used to guide nucleic coding sequence alignments with PAL2NAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609–W612 (2006)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2443">50</a></sup>. <i>Ks</i> values were calculated using the Yang–Nielson method implemented in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2450">51</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Phylogenomic analysis</h3><p>To infer the timing of genome duplication events relative to speciation events, all annotated <i>Musa</i> genes were sorted based on best BLASTP hit into the gene family clusters circumscribed by Jiao <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97–100 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2467">52</a></sup> and the PlantTribes database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wall, P. K. et al. PlantTribes: a gene and gene family resource for comparative genomics in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D970–D976 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2471">53</a></sup> (<a href="http://fgp.bio.psu.edu/tribedb/">http://fgp.bio.psu.edu/tribedb/</a>), including sequenced eudicotyledons and monocotyledons, along with transcriptome assemblies for other non-grass monocotyledons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>). Gene family clusters were queried for <i>Sorghum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J. E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2488">18</a></sup> and <i>Musa</i> orthologues mapping to syntenic blocks, and maximum likelihood gene trees were estimated for these gene families using the GTR+GAMMA model of molecular evolution in RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2495">54</a></sup>. The estimation of divergence times was performed on maximum likelihood trees based on concatenated MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Katoh, K., Asimenos, G. &amp; Toh, H. Multiple alignment of DNA sequences with MAFFT. Methods Mol. Biol. 537, 39–64 (2009)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2499">55</a></sup> alignments for 93 gene families that included only one gene from each of the sequenced genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Comparative analysis of gene families</h3><p>The <i>Musa</i> proteome was globally compared with <i>O. sativa</i> (RGAP version 6.0), <i>S. bicolor</i> (JGI version 1.4), <i>B. distachyon</i> (JGI version 1.0), <i>P. dactylifera</i> (draft sequence version 3, <a href="http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome/download.html">http://qatar-weill.cornell.edu/research/datepalmGenome/download.html</a>) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR version 9) proteomes filtered of transposable elements and alternative splicing. An all-against-all comparison was performed using BLASTP (1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>) followed by clustering with OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J., Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2542">56</a></sup> (inflation 1.5). Analysis of species-specific sets was made with a Fisher’s exact test (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001) on InterPro (version 28) domains. For analyses of specific gene families, the 36,542 <i>Musa</i> protein sequences were inserted in the plant proteome clustering of the GreenPhyl database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Rouard, M. et al. GreenPhylDB v2.0: comparative and functional genomics in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D1095–D1102 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2553">57</a></sup>. Transcription factor families were mostly retrieved based on InterPro domains, using the IPR2genomes tool in GreenphylDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Rouard, M. et al. GreenPhylDB v2.0: comparative and functional genomics in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D1095–D1102 (2011)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2557">57</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>). Kinases and nucleotide-binding site proteins were retrieved using hidden markov models (hmmsearch version 3) to search for corresponding Pfam domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Identification of CNSs</h3><p>Pan-grass CNSs conserved between rice, sorghum and <i>Brachypodium</i> were prepared using an automated pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Woodhouse, M. R. et al. Following tetraploidy in maize, a short deletion mechanism removed genes preferentially from one of the two homologs. PLoS Biol. 8, e1000409 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2579">58</a></sup>. The obtained 16,978 CNSs were used to query <i>Musa</i> using BLATSN (e value &lt; 0.001) following a manual or a semi-automated procedure depending on CNS size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>). The resulting set of CNSs was extensively analysed using GEvo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2593">59</a></sup> (<a href="http://synteny.cnr.berkeley.edu/CoGe/GEvo.pl">http://synteny.cnr.berkeley.edu/CoGe/GEvo.pl</a>) and the MSU Rice Genome Browser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Ouyang, S. et al. The TIGR Rice Genome Annotation Resource: improvements and new features. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, D883–D887 (2007)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2604">60</a></sup> (<a href="http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/cgi-bin/gbrowse/rice/">http://rice.plantbiology.msu.edu/cgi-bin/gbrowse/rice/</a>) to remove false positives (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Table 19</a>). Adding rice and sorghum homeologues, <i>Brachypodium</i> and maize orthologues and <i>Arabidopsis</i> ‘best hit orthologues’ to GEvo panels enabled the identification of 18 CNSs conserved deeply throughout the plant kingdom.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>For RNA-Seq analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature11241#MOESM254">Supplementary Text</a>), cDNA libraries were sequenced using 76-base length read chemistry in a single-flow cell on the Illumina GA IIx. Reads were mapped against the automatic annotated transcripts with SOAPaligner/Soap2 (2.20, <a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>) and only the unique mapped reads were kept. RNA-seq data were statistically analysed with the R packages baySeq version 1.6.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Hardcastle, T. &amp; Kelly, K. baySeq: empirical Bayesian methods for identifying differential expression in sequence count data. BMC Bioinformatics 11, 422 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2642">61</a>) and DESeq version 1.5.6 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 11, R106 (2010)" href="/articles/nature11241#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d29429e2645">62</a>).</p></div></div></section>

                    <li class="c-article-data-availability-list__item">
                      <p>
                        <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=HE813975%96HE813985">HE813975–HE813985</a>
                      </p>
                    </li>
                  </ul>
                
              
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Data deposits</h3>
                <p>The final assembly and annotation are deposited in DDBJ/EMBL/ GenBank under accession numbers <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=CAIC01000001%96CAIC01024424">CAIC01000001–CAIC01024424</a> (contigs), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=HE806462%96HE813974">HE806462–HE813974</a> (scaffolds) and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=HE813975%96HE813985">HE813975–HE813985</a> (chromosomes). Genome sequence and annotation can be obtained and viewed at <a href="http://banana-genome.cirad.fr">http://banana-genome.cirad.fr</a>.</p>

J.P., K.L., J.M.A. and M.B. performed sequencing and assembly. S.Br. performed genome size evaluation. F.C.B., N.R. and G.K. built or gave access to BAC libraries or BAC-end sequences. B.N., S.Bo., F.D., M.Co., J.Len., C.D.S., G.D., M.Ro., N.Y., F.C.B. and V.G. performed protein coding gene annotation. F.C., F.C.B., C.C., M.S., G.D., R.H., R.R., P.F., A.K., C.Je., F.B., S.J., M.R.G. and A.M.R. performed plant material development, ploidy analysis, DNA extraction, markers development, genotyping, genetic mapping, anchoring. K.J. performed gene GC content analyses. S.Bo., O.G., T.W., E.H., P.H.H., J.B., M.R.G., D.Burt., A.D.H., M.J., C.P., J.D., O.P., J.Len., G.D. and N.Y. performed transposable-element analysis. O.G., F.D., A.D.H., J.M.A., G.D., F.C.B., E.L., S.Bo. and O.J. performed WGD analyses based on synteny conservation. J.L.M., S.A., M.R.M., A.D.H., O.G. performed phylogenomic analyses of WGD. N.Y., M.Ro., J.Len., S.Bo., C.Jo., A.D., F.D., M.Ru. and A.Alm. performed gene family analyses. J.Lec., X.A., G.D. and S.Bo. performed transfer RNA and microRNA analyses. F.C.B. and M.Ch. performed endogenous virus analyses. D.Burg. and M.F. performed CNS analyses. N.Y., C.Jo., C.D.S., A.Alb., F.C., D.M.M., M.D., C.W., G.K., M.S., performed RNA extraction, phenotyping and/or transcriptomic analyses. A.D.H., N.Y., O.G., F.C., F.C.B., F.D., J.M.A., J.C.G., P.W., S.Bo., F.Q. and J.W. wrote or revised the paper. A.D.H., N.Y. and P.W. conceived and coordinated the whole project.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature11241/email/correspondent/c1/new">Angélique D’Hont</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature11241/email/correspondent/c2/new">Patrick Wincker</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Banana genome-sequence analysis</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>Bananas (<i>Musa</i> spp.) are a staple food and a major source of income in many tropical and subtropical countries. This paper reports the sequencing and analysis of the banana genome. This is the first non-grass monocotyledon to have its genome sequenced, providing an important bridge for comparative genome analysis in plants. Global banana production is under threat from increasingly well-adapted pests and diseases, so the availability of the genome sequence is an important resource for future crop development and improvement.</p></div></section></div></aside>
